<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Georgian_falconers_with_sparrowhawks.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The Eurasian Sparrowhawk is popular with <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">falconers</a> in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Eurasian Sparrowhawk has been used in <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">falconry</a> for centuries and was favoured by Emperor <a href="/wiki/Akbar_the_Great" title="Akbar the Great" class="mw-redirect">Akbar the Great</a> (1542–1605) of the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a>. There is a tradition of using migrant Eurasian Sparrowhawks to catch <a href="/wiki/Common_Quail" title="Common Quail">Common Quail</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, where there are 500 registered <i>bazieri</i> (sparrowhawkers) and a monument to <i>bazieri</i> in the city of <a href="/wiki/Poti" title="Poti">Poti</a>. Eurasian Sparrowhawks are also popular in <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Cap_Bon" title="Cap Bon">Cap Bon</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, thousands are captured each year by falconers and used for hunting migrant <a href="/wiki/Common_Quail" title="Common Quail">Common Quails</a>. Although they were formerly released at the end of the season, many are now kept because of the scarcity of migrants.<sup id="cite_ref-ibc92_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ibc92-56"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In England in the 17th century, the Eurasian Sparrowhawk was used by priests, reflecting their lowly status;<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> whereas in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, they were favoured by ladies of noble and royal status because of their small size.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> The falconer's name for a male Eurasian Sparrowhawk is a "musket"; this is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word <i>musca</i>, meaning 'a fly', via the <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> word <i>moschet</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> The famous list in the mediaeval <a href="/wiki/Book_of_St._Albans" title="Book of St. Albans" class="mw-redirect">Book of St. Albans</a> refers to the female sparrowhawk as the "priest's bird" and the musket as "the clerk's bird".</p>
<p><i>"An austringer [falconer] undertaking to train a sparrowhawk should be in doubt that he is taking on one of the most difficult hawks available."</i> A female Eurasian Sparrowhawk is considered a bad choice for a novice and the male is very difficult and demanding, even for an experienced handler. They have been described as <i>"hysterical little hawks</i>" but are also praised as courageous and providing <i>"sport of the highest quality."</i><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> Philip Glasier describes Eurasian Sparrowhawks as "<i>in many ways superior to hunting with a larger short-wing</i> [hawk]" and <i>"extremely hard to tame."</i> They are best suited for small quarry such as <a href="/wiki/Common_Starling" title="Common Starling">Common Starlings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Common_Blackbird" title="Common Blackbird">Common Blackbirds</a> but are also capable of taking <a href="/wiki/Common_Teal" title="Common Teal" class="mw-redirect">Common Teal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Magpie" title="Eurasian Magpie">Eurasian Magpies</a>, pheasants and partridges.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> A 19th-century author remarked that this species was <i>"the best of all hawks for landrails"</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-penny_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-penny-63"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> now known as <a href="/wiki/Corn_Crake" title="Corn Crake">Corn Crakes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1735, the <i>Sportsman's Dictionary</i> noted that <i>"... she will serve in the winter as well as in the summer, and will fly at all kind of game more than the falcon. If a winter sparrow-hawk prove good, she will kill the <a href="/wiki/European_Magpie" title="European Magpie" class="mw-redirect">pye</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Red-billed_Chough" title="Red-billed Chough">chough</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Jay" title="Eurasian Jay">jay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Woodcock" title="Eurasian Woodcock">woodcock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turdus" title="Turdus" class="mw-redirect">thrush</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_Blackbird" title="Common Blackbird">black-bird</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fieldfare" title="Fieldfare">fieldfare</a>, and divers[e] other birds of the like nature."</i><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_Sparrowhawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: In culture">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="In_culture">In culture</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_nisus_1_(Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Adult male Eurasian Sparrowhawk perching on branch" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Accipiter_nisus_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg/220px-Accipiter_nisus_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Accipiter_nisus_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg/330px-Accipiter_nisus_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Accipiter_nisus_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg/440px-Accipiter_nisus_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_nisus_1_(Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
An adult male in Slovakia</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Teutonic_mythology" title="Teutonic mythology" class="mw-redirect">Teutonic mythology</a>, the sparrowhawk, known as <i>krahui</i> or <i>krahug</i>, is a sacred bird in Old Bohemian songs and lives in a grove of the gods. Holy sparrowhawks perch on the branches of an oak tree that grows from the grave of a murdered man, and "<i>publish the foul deed.</i>"<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> In some areas of England, it was believed that the <a href="/wiki/Common_Cuckoo" title="Common Cuckoo">Common Cuckoo</a> turned into a Eurasian Sparrowhawk in winter.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Surname" title="Surname">name</a> <i>Spearhafoc</i> (later Sparhawk, Sparrowhawk) was in use as a personal name in England before the <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">Norman conquest</a> in 1066.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1695, John Aubrey wrote in his <i>Miscellanies":<sup id="cite_ref-lore_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lore-3"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup></i></p>
<blockquote>
<p>'Not long before the Death of King Charles II a sparrow-hawk escaped from the Perch, and pitched on one of the Iron Crowns of the White Tower, and entangling its string in the Crown, hung by the heels and died. T'was considered very ominous, and so it proved.'</p>
</blockquote>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Musket" title="Musket">musket</a>, or musquet, originally a kind of <a href="/wiki/Crossbow" title="Crossbow">crossbow</a> bolt,<sup id="cite_ref-brit_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brit-57"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> and later a small <a href="/wiki/Cannon" title="Cannon">cannon</a>, was named after the male Eurasian Sparrowhawk because of its size.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> The British <a href="/wiki/Gloster_Aircraft_Company" title="Gloster Aircraft Company">Gloster Aircraft Company</a> named one of their Mars series craft<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Gloster_Sparrowhawk" title="Gloster Sparrowhawk">Sparrowhawk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-brit_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brit-57"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Merry_Wives_of_Windsor" title="The Merry Wives of Windsor">The Merry Wives of Windsor</a></i>, Mrs Ford greets Robin, <a href="/wiki/Falstaff" title="Falstaff">Falstaff</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Page_(servant)" title="Page (servant)">page</a>, with the words <i>"How now, my eyas musket"</i>, <i>eyas musket</i> meaning a lively young man (an eyas is a hawk nestling).<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> The British <a href="/wiki/Poet_Laureate" title="Poet Laureate" class="mw-redirect">Poet Laureate</a> <a href="/wiki/Ted_Hughes" title="Ted Hughes">Ted Hughes</a> wrote a poem entitled <i>Sparrow Hawk</i> which refers to this species.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Hesse" title="Hermann Hesse">Hermann Hesse</a> mentioned this bird in his book <i><a href="/wiki/Demian" title="Demian">Demian</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> and the bird is also referred to in <a href="/wiki/One_Thousand_and_One_Arabian_Nights" title="One Thousand and One Arabian Nights" class="mw-redirect">One Thousand and One Arabian Nights</a> by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Francis_Burton" title="Richard Francis Burton">Richard Francis Burton</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<blockquote>
<p>Good sooth my bones, wheneas they hear thy name<br />
Quail as birds quailed when Nisus o'er them flew<br /></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The Eurasian Sparrowhawk was written about by <a href="/wiki/Alfred,_Lord_Tennyson" title="Alfred, Lord Tennyson">Alfred, Lord Tennyson</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-lore_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lore-3"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<blockquote>
<p>A sparhawk proud did hold in wicked jail<br />
Music's sweet chorister, the Nightingale<br />
To whom with sighs she said: 'O set me free,<br />
And in my song I'll praise no bird but thee.'<br />
The Hawk replied: 'I will not lose my diet<br />
To let a thousand such enjoy their quiet.'<br /></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_Sparrowhawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist references-column-count references-column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2; list-style-type: decimal;">
